## Session 7: Guest (General)

**5** turn(s) · 04 Jun 2026 · `chat_1780566730365_zkh8i67` · page: /counseling/tnea-barch/counselling-procedure

### Turn 1 · 04 Jun 2026 15:23 IST

**Student asked:**

> Hi

**Aintra answered:**

> Hi there! Welcome to Neram Classes. How can I help you today with your NATA or JEE Paper 2 preparation, or any questions about architecture colleges? 😊

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 1476 ms_  

### Turn 2 · 04 Jun 2026 15:24 IST

**Student asked:**

> Now I have applied for josaa i doubt what on choice filling can I choose

**Aintra answered:**

> Great that you've applied for JoSAA! Choice filling is a crucial step. For B.Arch programs through JoSAA, you'll be able to choose from a variety of institutions including NITs (National Institutes of Technology), SPAs (Schools of Planning and Architecture), and some IIITs.
> 
> It's important to remember that for the IITs that offer B.Arch (IIT Roorkee, IIT Kharagpur, and IIT (BHU) Varanasi), you would also need to have qualified the **Architecture Aptitude Test (AAT)** in addition to JEE Advanced. If you've cleared AAT, you can include these IITs in your choices.
> 
> When filling your choices, here are some tips:
> *   **Research Colleges:** Look into the B.Arch programs offered by different NITs, SPAs, and IITs (if AAT qualified). Consider factors like location, faculty, infrastructure, and placement records.
> *   **Prioritize:** List your preferred colleges and programs in descending order of your preference. Put the colleges you want the most at the top, regardless of your rank, and then follow with others.
> *   **Consider all options:** Don't limit yourself to just a few choices. Fill as many options as you are genuinely interested in, as this increases your chances of getting an allotment.
> *   **Use official resources:** Always refer to the official JoSAA information brochure and website (josaa.nic.in) for the most accurate and up-to-date list of participating institutions, seat matrix, and detailed instructions on choice filling.
> 
> Do you have any specific colleges in mind, or are you looking for general advice on how to approach the choice-filling process?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 4867 ms_  

### Turn 3 · 04 Jun 2026 15:26 IST

**Student asked:**

> I have scored 74 percentage in jee mains and 12th board 382 of 600 and my rank in jee mains is 1190 can u help me with the choice filling in JoSAA counselling

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a good score in JEE Mains! With a rank of **1190**, you have a strong chance for B.Arch admissions through JoSAA.
> 
> While I can't directly fill out your choices or guarantee admission to a specific college (as JoSAA choice filling depends on many dynamic factors like category, home state, and opening/closing ranks which change each year), I can give you some general guidance and recommend resources to help you make informed decisions:
> 
> 1.  **Understand Your Eligibility:**
>     *   **JEE Main Paper 2 Score:** This is the primary factor for NITs, SPAs, and some IIITs. Your rank of **1190** is competitive.
>     *   **12th Board Marks:** Your 12th board marks (382 out of 600) are also considered for eligibility, ensuring you meet the minimum percentage criteria set by the respective institutions.
> 
> 2.  **Research Colleges Thoroughly:**
>     *   **NITs, SPAs, and IIITs:** These are the main institutions that admit through JEE Paper 2. Look into the B.Arch programs at top-ranked NITs (like NIT Trichy, NIT Calicut, NIT Warangal), and the Schools of Planning and Architecture (SPAs) in Delhi, Bhopal, and Vijayawada.
>     *   **IITs (if AAT qualified):** If you also appeared for and qualified the **Architecture Aptitude Test (AAT)**, then IIT Roorkee, IIT Kharagpur, and IIT (BHU) Varanasi are also options. If not, these won't be available.
>     *   **Factors to consider:** When researching, look at NIRF rankings, faculty-student ratio, infrastructure, studio facilities, curriculum, and placement records. Our college hub at neramclasses.com/colleges can help you explore.
> 
> 3.  **Prioritize Your Choices:**
>     *   **Dream Colleges First:** List your most preferred colleges and programs at the top of your choice list, even if they seem ambitious.
>     *   **Realistic Options:** Follow with colleges where your rank is more likely to secure a seat, based on previous years' opening and closing ranks (which you can find on the JoSAA website or other counseling portals).
>     *   **Backup Options:** Include some colleges that are relatively safer bets to ensure you get an allotment.
> 
> 4.  **Use Official JoSAA Resources:**
>     *   The **JoSAA website (josaa.nic.in)** is your best friend. It provides the official information brochure, seat matrix, and previous years' opening and closing ranks. Analyzing these trends is crucial for strategic choice filling.
>     *   Participate in any mock allocation rounds if available, as they can give you an idea of where you stand.
> 
> 5.  **Consider NATA:** While you've focused on JEE Main, remember that the **NATA (National Aptitude Test in Architecture)** is also a primary entrance exam for B.Arch admissions into most state and private architecture colleges across India. Many students take both JEE Paper 2 and NATA to maximize their admission opportunities.
> 
> I recommend spending significant time on the JoSAA website to understand the previous year's cutoffs for your category and home state. This will give you the most accurate picture for your choice filling strategy.
> 
> Do you have any specific states or regions you are interested in, or particular types of institutions (e.g., NITs, SPAs)?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 5286 ms_  

### Turn 4 · 04 Jun 2026 15:27 IST

**Student asked:**

> I have scored 74 percentage in jee mains and 12th board 382 of 600 and my rank in jee mains is 1190 in sc catagory can u help me with the choice filling in JoSAA counselling

**Aintra answered:**

> That's fantastic news! With a JEE Main Paper 2 rank of **1190 in the SC category**, you have a very strong position for B.Arch admissions through JoSAA counseling. Your SC category reservation will significantly enhance your chances of securing a seat in a good institution.
> 
> Here’s how you can approach your JoSAA choice filling:
> 
> 1.  **Leverage Your SC Category Rank:** Your rank of **1190 in the SC category** is excellent. When you look at previous years' JoSAA cutoffs, you'll notice that the closing ranks for SC candidates are generally much higher than for the General category. This means you have a strong possibility of getting into highly sought-after NITs and SPAs.
> 
> 2.  **Focus on Top Institutions (NITs & SPAs):**
>     *   **NITs:** Explore B.Arch programs at top National Institutes of Technology like NIT Trichy, NIT Calicut, NIT Warangal, NIT Hamirpur, etc. These often have excellent architecture departments.
>     *   **SPAs:** The Schools of Planning and Architecture (SPA Delhi, SPA Bhopal, SPA Vijayawada) are premier institutions for architecture and planning. You should definitely include them in your top preferences.
>     *   **IIITs:** While fewer IIITs offer B.Arch, check if any align with your interests.
> 
> 3.  **IITs (AAT Qualification is Key):**
>     *   Remember, for B.Arch programs at IIT Roorkee, IIT Kharagpur, and IIT (BHU) Varanasi, you must have not only qualified JEE Advanced but also passed the **Architecture Aptitude Test (AAT)**. If you have cleared AAT, you should definitely include these IITs in your priority list, as they are among the most prestigious institutions.
> 
> 4.  **Strategic Choice Filling:**
>     *   **Ambitious Choices First:** Given your strong SC rank, don't hesitate to put your dream colleges at the very top of your list. You have a good chance.
>     *   **Broaden Your Options:** After your top preferences, include a wider range of NITs and SPAs where you have a very high probability of getting a seat based on past trends for the SC category.
>     *   **Home State vs. Other State Quota:** Be mindful of the home state and other state quotas, as these can influence your chances. Your rank will be considered under both, but often home state quotas can offer a slight advantage for colleges in your state.
> 
> 5.  **Importance of 12th Board Marks:** Your 12th board marks (382 out of 600) are important for meeting the minimum eligibility criteria for admission to B.Arch programs at these institutions. Make sure you meet the specific percentage requirements for each college you list.
> 
> 6.  **Official JoSAA Resources are Paramount:**
>     *   The **JoSAA website (josaa.nic.in)** is your primary and most reliable source. Crucially, look for the **previous years' opening and closing ranks specifically for the SC category**. This data will be your best guide for understanding which colleges you can realistically aim for.
>     *   Pay close attention to the seat matrix for B.Arch programs, which will detail the number of seats available in each category at each institution.
> 
> I strongly recommend you spend time analyzing the previous year's cutoffs for the SC category on the JoSAA portal. This will give you the most accurate picture of your prospects and help you create a highly effective choice list.
> 
> Do you have any specific regions or colleges you're particularly interested in, or perhaps any questions about how to interpret the previous year's cutoff data?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 5830 ms_  

### Turn 5 · 04 Jun 2026 15:28 IST

**Student asked:**

> Make a list

**Aintra answered:**

> Okay, with your **JEE Main Paper 2 rank of 1190 in the SC category** and your 12th board marks, here's a structured approach to building your choice list for JoSAA counseling, focusing on the top institutions:
> 
> **Key Institutions to Prioritize (Highly Recommended to Research and Include):**
> 
> 1.  **Schools of Planning and Architecture (SPAs):** These are considered the premier institutions for architecture in India through JoSAA. With your SC category rank, you have a very strong chance at these.
>     *   **SPA Delhi**
>     *   **SPA Bhopal**
>     *   **SPA Vijayawada**
> 
> 2.  **National Institutes of Technology (NITs) with B.Arch Programs:** Many NITs offer excellent B.Arch courses. Given your rank, you should aim for the top-tier NITs.
>     *   **NIT Tiruchirappalli (Trichy)**
>     *   **NIT Calicut**
>     *   **NIT Warangal**
>     *   **NIT Hamirpur**
>     *   **Malaviya National Institute of Technology (MNIT) Jaipur**
>     *   **Visvesvaraya National Institute of Technology (VNIT) Nagpur**
>     *   **Maulana Azad National Institute of Technology (MANIT) Bhopal**
>     *   **National Institute of Technology Patna**
>     *   **National Institute of Technology Raipur**
>     *   **National Institute of Technology Rourkela**
>     *   **National Institute of Technology Agartala**
> 
> 3.  **Indian Institutes of Technology (IITs) - *If AAT Qualified***:
>     *   **IIT Roorkee**
>     *   **IIT Kharagpur**
>     *   **IIT (BHU) Varanasi**
>     *   **Important:** You can only include these if you have successfully cleared the **Architecture Aptitude Test (AAT)**. If you haven't, these will not be options for you.
> 
> **How to Build Your Personalized List for Choice Filling:**
> 
> 1.  **Start Ambitiously:** Begin your list with the SPAs and the top NITs, placing your absolute dream colleges at the very top. Even if they seem a bit out of reach for general category, your SC rank significantly improves your chances.
> 
> 2.  **Broaden Your Options:** Continue adding other well-regarded NITs that offer B.Arch programs. Use the official JoSAA website to find the complete list of participating institutions and their B.Arch offerings.
> 
> 3.  **Review Previous Years' Cutoffs (SC Category):** This is the **most critical step** for a successful strategy.
>     *   Go to the official **JoSAA website (josaa.nic.in)**.
>     *   Look for "Opening and Closing Ranks" from previous years.
>     *   Filter by "JEE Main Paper 2" and specifically by "SC Category" (and "Home State" vs. "Other State" if applicable).
>     *   Identify colleges where your rank of **1190** falls within or is very close to the closing ranks for the SC category in previous years. This will give you a realistic idea of your chances for each institution.
> 
> 4.  **Consider Location and Other Factors:** While rank is important, also think about:
>     *   **Location:** Do you have a preference for a specific region or city?
>     *   **Curriculum & Faculty:** Research the specific B.Arch program details on the college websites.
>     *   **Infrastructure:** Look into the studio facilities, labs, and overall campus environment.
> 
> **Important Reminders:**
> 
> *   **Official JoSAA Data is King:** My list above is a general guide. Your final choice filling list should be primarily informed by the **official JoSAA opening and closing ranks for the SC category** from previous years. This data is the most accurate predictor.
> *   **Fill Many Choices:** Don't limit yourself. Fill as many choices as you are genuinely interested in to maximize your chances of getting an allotment.
> *   **Verify Eligibility:** Ensure you meet the minimum 12th board percentage criteria for each college you list.
> 
> By following this structured approach and carefully analyzing the JoSAA data for the SC category, you'll be well-equipped to make informed choices!
> 
> Do you have any questions about specific colleges or how to navigate the JoSAA website for cutoff data?

## Session 9: Guest (General)

**1** turn(s) · 02 Jun 2026 · `chat_1780407242052_dgax8g1` · page: /counseling/kea-barch

### Turn 1 · 02 Jun 2026 19:05 IST

**Student asked:**

> Does kea accept JEE barch score

**Aintra answered:**

> For B.Arch admissions in Karnataka, KEA primarily uses **KCET** for government and aided seats, and **COMEDK** for private college seats. However, a valid **NATA score is mandatory** for B.Arch admission through both KCET and COMEDK.
> 
> JEE Paper 2 scores are generally used for admissions to NITs, SPAs, and IIITs through JoSAA counseling, not typically by KEA for colleges in Karnataka.
> 
> Do you have any specific colleges in mind that you'd like to know more about?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2769 ms_

> 
> *   **Conducted by:** Council of Architecture (CoA)
> *   **Exam Pattern:** It's a 3-hour exam for 200 marks. It has two parts:
>     *   **Part A (Drawing):** 80 marks, offline (pen and paper).
>     *   **Part B (MCQ/NCQ):** 120 marks, online adaptive (multiple-choice and numerical answer questions).
> *   **Attempts:** NATA 2026 will have two phases. Phase 1 runs from **April 4 to June 13, 2026** (up to 2 attempts, percentile scoring), and Phase 2 is on **August 7-8, 2026** (1 attempt, raw scores). You can only appear in one phase.
> *   **No Negative Marking:** That's good news!
> *   **Eligibility:** You need to have passed 10+2 with Physics and Mathematics as compulsory subjects, with a minimum of 45% aggregate marks for admission to B.Arch.

### Turn 2 · 30 May 2026 13:56 IST

**Student asked:**

> Tell me more and give a a list of colleges where I have chance to get in

**Aintra answered:**

> You're looking for specific college recommendations based on your scores, and I understand that! However, my tools don't allow me to filter colleges by exact NATA scores or 12th board percentages directly. Admission cutoffs can also change each year based on the number of applicants and their performance.
> 
> That's why I strongly recommend using our **free College Predictor tool** at app.neramclasses.com. It's designed to give you a personalized list of colleges where you might have a chance based on your NATA score and other academic details.
> 
> However, I can tell you more about how admissions generally work in Tamil Nadu, Karnataka, and Kerala for B.Arch:
> 
> ### **Tamil Nadu (TNEA B.Arch Counseling)**
> *   **Merit Calculation:** Your B.Arch merit score for TNEA is calculated by combining your 12th board marks (scaled to 200) and your NATA score (out of 200), for a total out of 400. The higher your combined score, the better your rank.
> *   **NATA Requirement:** A valid NATA score is mandatory.
> *   **Counseling:** Admissions are through a centralized online counseling process (TNEA B.Arch) conducted by Anna University. You register, upload documents, and then participate in choice filling and allotment rounds.
> *   **Colleges:** Includes Anna University departments, government-aided colleges, and self-financing colleges that surrender seats to TNEA.
> 
> ### **Kerala (KEAM B.Arch Counseling)**
> *   **Merit Calculation:** For B.Arch, KEAM gives equal weightage to your NATA score (scaled to 200) and your 10+2 marks (scaled to 200), for a total rank index out of 400.
> *   **NATA Requirement:** Only NATA 2025 or NATA 2026 scores are accepted. KEAM does not conduct a separate entrance exam for B.Arch.
> *   **Counseling:** Admissions are through the Centralized Allotment Process (CAP) conducted by CEE Kerala.
> *   **Colleges:** Primarily self-financing colleges affiliated with various state universities.
> 
> ### **Karnataka (KCET / COMEDK / Management Quota)**
> *   **NATA Requirement:** A valid NATA score is essential for B.Arch admission in most colleges.
> *   **Other Exams:** For government and aided seats, KCET (Karnataka Common Entrance Test) is used. For private self-financing colleges, COMEDK is often involved, or you might apply through management quota.
> *   **Counseling:** Admissions are managed by KEA (Karnataka Examinations Authority) for KCET, and COMEDK conducts its own counseling.
> 
> With a NATA score of 88 out of 200, it's important to be realistic about the highly competitive colleges. You might find better opportunities in some self-financing or newer institutions.
> 
> Again, I highly recommend trying our College Predictor. It's the best way to see which colleges might be a good fit for your scores!
> 
> Do you have any other questions about the admission process in these states?

### Turn 2 · 29 May 2026 09:28 IST

**Student asked:**

> Yes

**Aintra answered:**

> Great! Let's dive into the KEAM B.Arch admission process.
> 
> For B.Arch admissions in Kerala, KEAM doesn't conduct a separate entrance exam. Instead, your rank is calculated by giving equal weightage to your **NATA score (out of 200)** and your **10+2 marks (out of 200)**, for a total index out of 400.
> 
> Here are some key points about KEAM B.Arch:
> *   **Academic Eligibility:** You need to have passed 10+2 with Physics and Mathematics as compulsory subjects, plus one elective, with a minimum of **45% aggregate marks**. A 10+3 Diploma with Mathematics and 45% marks is also accepted.
> *   **NATA Score:** Only NATA 2025 or NATA 2026 scores are accepted, and you must meet the minimum eligibility marks set by the Council of Architecture.
> *   **Age Limit:** You must be at least **17 years old by December 31, 2026**.
> *   **Application Process:** The entire application, fee payment, document upload, and option registration happen online at **cee.kerala.gov.in**. Online applications for KEAM 2026 are expected to open around **January 5, 2026**, and close by **January 31, 2026**.
> 
> The Centralised Allotment Process (CAP) typically runs a Trial allotment, Phase 1, Phase 2, and sometimes a Mop-up round. You must register your options before each phase to be considered for seat allotment.
> 
> Is there anything specific you'd like to know about the KEAM process, like fee waivers, tie-breaking rules, or required documents?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2284 ms_  

### Turn 3 · 23 May 2026 10:57 IST

**Student asked:**

> And its showing for 75 marks i am in top 85%

**Aintra answered:**

> That's an interesting observation, and it highlights how **percentile scores** work!
> 
> When you see "top 75%" for a score of 83 and "top 85%" for a score of 75, it means that these results are likely from **different exam attempts or different cohorts of students**.
> 
> Here's why:
> 
> *   **Percentiles are relative:** Your percentile rank depends on how you performed compared to **everyone else who took that specific exam attempt**.
> *   **Varying difficulty:** If one exam attempt was significantly harder, a lower raw score (like 75) could still place you in a higher percentile (like top 85%) because most other students also scored lower. Conversely, if an exam was easier, a higher raw score (like 83) might place you in a slightly lower percentile (like top 75%) because more students scored well.
> 
> So, it's not about the raw score alone, but how that score stacks up against the performance of others in that particular NATA attempt.
> 
> Do you have any questions about how these scores are used in college admissions, or would you like to know more about the NATA exam structure?

## Session 35: Guest (General)

**5** turn(s) · 19 May 2026 · `chat_1779178293662_8qse4r1` · page: /counseling/tnea-barch/faq

### Turn 1 · 19 May 2026 13:44 IST

**Student asked:**

> In BArch application there is place to enter both JEE BArch score and NATA score.  Can I enter both? How will they consider my marks for the rank? How is the merit score calculated?

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great question about how your scores are considered for B.Arch admissions!
> 
> Yes, if you have both a JEE Main Paper 2 B.Arch score and a NATA score, you can definitely enter both in your application. The counseling authority will typically consider the **higher of the two scores** (NATA or JEE Paper 2) when calculating your merit.
> 
> For the merit score calculation, especially in systems like TNEA B.Arch counseling, your marks are generally weighted as follows:
> 
> *   Your 10+2 (or equivalent) board marks are scaled to a total of 200.
> *   Your NATA or JEE Main Paper 2 score (whichever is higher) is also considered out of 200.
> *   These two scores are then combined to give you a total merit score out of 400.
> 
> So, the formula looks like this: **Total Merit Score = (10+2 Board Marks scaled to 200) + (Higher of NATA/JEE Paper 2 score scaled to 200)**. This ensures that both your academic performance and your architectural aptitude are equally considered.
> 
> Do you have any specific state counseling in mind, like TNEA (Tamil Nadu Engineering Admissions), or are you exploring options more broadly?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 4147 ms_  

### Turn 2 · 19 May 2026 13:45 IST

**Student asked:**

> Will TNEA do that by itself?

**Aintra answered:**

> Yes, for TNEA B.Arch counseling, the system is designed to automatically consider the higher of your NATA or JEE Main Paper 2 scores. You just need to ensure both scores are correctly entered during the online registration process.
> 
> When the TNEA merit list is prepared, they will take your 10+2 academic marks (scaled to 200) and add it to the better of your NATA or JEE Paper 2 score (also scaled to 200), resulting in a total merit score out of 400. This ensures you get the maximum benefit from your aptitude exam performance!
> 
> Is there anything else you'd like to know about the TNEA process?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2342 ms_

### Turn 7 · 19 May 2026 10:35 IST

**Student asked:**

> How is the merit score calculated if I use my JEE score

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great question! For TNEA B.Arch admissions, if you use your JEE (Main) Paper-2 score, the merit score is calculated out of a total of **400 marks**.
> 
> Here's how it breaks down:
> 
> 1.  **Your 12th-grade (HSC) Board Marks:** Your aggregate marks from your 12th standard (or equivalent) are scaled to a maximum of **200 marks**. This is typically done by taking your total marks, dividing them by the maximum possible marks, and then multiplying by 200.
> 2.  **Your JEE (Main) Paper-2 Score:** Your JEE Paper-2 score is also considered out of a maximum of **200 marks**.
> 
> The final TNEA B.Arch merit score is the sum of these two components: **(Scaled 12th-grade marks out of 200) + (JEE Paper-2 score out of 200) = Total Merit Score out of 400.**
> 
> If you happen to have both a NATA score and a JEE Paper-2 score, TNEA will automatically consider the **higher of the two** for your merit calculation, ensuring you get the best possible advantage.
> 
> This combined score is what determines your rank in the TNEA B.Arch merit list, which is then used for college allotments.
> 
> Does that explanation clarify how your JEE score contributes to your overall merit?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 3138 ms_  

### Turn 8 · 19 May 2026 10:36 IST

**Student asked:**

> Suppose if I have 81 percent board marks and 98 percentile in JEE. How the calculation will be done

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great hypothetical scenario to understand the TNEA B.Arch merit calculation! Here's how it would generally work:
> 
> The TNEA B.Arch merit score is calculated out of a total of **400 marks**, with equal weightage given to your 12th-grade marks and your JEE (Main) Paper-2 score.
> 
> Let's break down your scenario:
> 
> 1.  **Your 12th-grade Board Marks:**
>     *   You have **81%** in your board exams.
>     *   This percentage is scaled to **200 marks**.
>     *   Calculation: `(81 / 100) * 200 = 162 marks`
> 
> 2.  **Your JEE (Main) Paper-2 Score:**
>     *   You mentioned a **98 percentile** in JEE Paper-2.
>     *   **Important Note:** The TNEA calculation uses your *actual JEE Paper-2 score out of 200* (after NTA's scaling, as JEE Paper-2 is originally out of 400, but for TNEA it's considered out of 200), not directly the percentile. While a 98 percentile is excellent, I cannot convert it into a precise score out of 200 without knowing the exact scoring methodology used by TNEA for percentiles, or your raw score from NTA. The percentile tells us your relative rank among all test-takers, but not the absolute score TNEA will use.
> 
> **So, the calculation would be:**
> 
> *   **12th Board Marks Component:** **162 marks**
> *   **JEE Paper-2 Score Component:** *(Let's assume for illustration that your 98 percentile corresponds to, say, 180 marks out of 200 in the TNEA's internal JEE score conversion)*
>     *   *If it were 180 marks:* **180 marks**
> 
> *   **Total Illustrative Merit Score:** `162 (Board) + 180 (JEE) = 342 marks out of 400`
> 
> This **342 marks** would be your merit score, which would then determine your rank in the TNEA B.Arch rank list. A score in this range would typically place you very competitively for good colleges.
> 
> To get the most accurate picture, you'd need to know the exact score (out of 200) that TNEA considers from your JEE Main Paper-2 result.

**Aintra answered:**

> Yes, that's correct! TNEA (or any other counseling authority) will use your **official JEE (Main) Paper-2 score** as declared by the National Testing Agency (NTA).
> 
> Here's the clarification:
> 
> *   **NTA provides your JEE Paper-2 score:** When you get your JEE Main Paper-2 result, NTA provides you with a score (which is typically out of 400 for the B.Arch paper) and a percentile.
> *   **TNEA uses the score, not the percentile:** For the TNEA B.Arch merit list, they will take your actual JEE Paper-2 score. Since their merit calculation requires a component out of **200 marks** for the entrance exam, TNEA will have its own established method to scale or consider your NTA-provided JEE Paper-2 score to fit this 200-mark slot. They are essentially doing an internal conversion of your *score* to align with their 200-mark component.
> 
> So, you don't need to do any conversion yourself. Just ensure you have your official JEE Main Paper-2 scorecard, and TNEA's system will handle how that score is integrated into their 400-mark merit calculation.

> *   **No Public Formula:** This conversion process is part of their official guidelines and may involve factors beyond a simple division, potentially considering the distribution of scores, etc. It's not something you need to calculate manually.

### Turn 1 · 17 May 2026 16:56 IST

**Student asked:**

> tnea barch eligibility do you know ?

**Aintra answered:**

> Yes, I can help you with the TNEA B.Arch eligibility criteria!
> 
> To be eligible for TNEA B.Arch counseling, you generally need to meet these requirements:
> 
> *   **Academic Qualification:** You must have passed 10+2 (or an equivalent exam) with Physics and Mathematics as compulsory subjects. You also need one elective subject from options like Chemistry, Biology, Computer Science, IT, Engineering Graphics, Business Studies, or a Technical Vocational subject. You need to score a minimum of **45% aggregate marks** in these subjects. A 10+3 Diploma with Mathematics as a compulsory subject and 45% aggregate marks is also accepted.
> *   **Entrance Exam:** You need a qualifying score in either **NATA** (National Aptitude Test in Architecture) or **JEE (Main) Paper-2 B.Arch**. If you have scores from both, the higher score will be considered for merit calculation.
> 
> The official portal for TNEA B.Arch is **https://barch.tneaonline.org**.
>

## Session 49: Guest (General)

**3** turn(s) · 15 May 2026 · `chat_1778827786372_jdfuxeq` · page: /achievements

### Turn 1 · 15 May 2026 12:21 IST

**Student asked:**

> syllabus nata 2026

**Aintra answered:**

> NATA 2026 has two main parts:
> 
> *   **Part A (Drawing):** This section is offline (pen and paper) and carries **80 marks**. It assesses your drawing skills, observation, sense of proportion, and aesthetic sensitivity.
> *   **Part B (MCQ/NCQ):** This section is online and adaptive, carrying **120 marks**. It covers topics like visual reasoning, logical deduction, general knowledge, architectural awareness, and mental ability.
> 
> The total marks for the exam are **200**, and the duration is **3 hours**. There is no negative marking, and no minimum qualifying score is prescribed.
> 
> For more detailed information, you can visit www.nata.in or explore our free tools at app.neramclasses.com which include mock tests to help you prepare!

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2621 ms_  

### Turn 2 · 15 May 2026 12:21 IST

**Student asked:**

> maths syllabus

**Aintra answered:**

> The NATA 2026 exam does not have a separate, explicit "Maths syllabus" like a traditional academic subject. Instead, the mathematical aptitude is assessed as part of the **Part B (MCQ/NCQ)** section, which covers:
> 
> *   **Logical Deduction:** This involves problem-solving using logical reasoning, often with numerical or spatial components.
> *   **Visual Reasoning:** This can include questions related to patterns, sequences, and spatial arrangements where mathematical concepts like geometry and symmetry might be indirectly tested.
> *   **Mental Ability:** This broadly covers quantitative aptitude, including basic arithmetic, percentages, ratios, and data interpretation.
> 
> While there isn't a specific list of mathematical topics like algebra or calculus, a strong foundation in **basic arithmetic, geometry, and logical reasoning** will be beneficial.
> 
> Do you have any other questions about the NATA syllabus or preparation?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 1875 ms_  

### Turn 3 · 15 May 2026 12:22 IST

**Student asked:**

> in detailed math syllabus

**Aintra answered:**

> You're looking for a more detailed breakdown of the mathematical concepts that might appear in the NATA exam. While NATA doesn't publish a specific "Mathematics syllabus" in the way board exams do, the quantitative and logical reasoning abilities are certainly tested. These are primarily integrated into the **Part B (MCQ/NCQ)** section, under categories like Logical Deduction, Visual Reasoning, and Mental Ability.
> 
> Based on the nature of aptitude tests, here are the types of mathematical concepts you can expect:
> 
> 1.  **Basic Arithmetic:**
>     *   Numbers (integers, fractions, decimals)
>     *   Percentages, Ratios, and Proportions
>     *   Averages
>     *   Profit and Loss
>     *   Time and Work
>     *   Simple Equations and Problem Solving
> 
> 2.  **Basic Geometry and Spatial Aptitude:**
>     *   Properties of 2D shapes (squares, rectangles, circles, triangles, polygons) – calculating area, perimeter.
>     *   Properties of 3D shapes (cubes, cuboids, cylinders, cones, spheres) – calculating volume, surface area.
>     *   Understanding angles, lines, and basic geometric theorems.
>     *   Spatial visualization: mentally manipulating 2D and 3D objects, identifying nets of solids, counting surfaces.
>     *   Symmetry and transformations.
> 
> 3.  **Logical and Analytical Reasoning (with numerical elements):**
>     *   Number Series and Sequences (identifying patterns)
>     *   Coding and Decoding (often involving numerical logic)
>     *   Data Interpretation (reading and understanding information from graphs, charts, and tables)
>     *   Blood relations, directions, seating arrangements (which sometimes involve numerical positions or counts).
> 
> The focus is less on complex formulas and advanced mathematics, and more on your ability to apply basic mathematical principles to solve problems, interpret data, and reason logically.
> 
> To get the best understanding of the types of questions asked, I highly recommend practicing with NATA mock tests and sample papers. You can find free tools and mock tests at **app.neramclasses.com**. This will give you a practical idea of how these mathematical concepts are presented in the exam.

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 5687 ms_

### Turn 5 · 13 May 2026 22:48 IST

**Student asked:**

> Ok i have seen the youtube video about the syllabus ok nata can you tell the syllabus of jee paper 2 I need like what is the mark

**Aintra answered:**

> Certainly! It's great you're exploring both NATA and JEE Paper 2.
> 
> For **JEE Paper 2 (B.Arch/B.Planning)**, the exam is divided into three parts:
> 
> 1.  **Part I: Mathematics** - This section contains 30 questions (20 Multiple Choice Questions and 10 Numerical Answer Type questions, out of which 5 have to be attempted). It carries **100 marks**.
> 2.  **Part II: Aptitude Test** - This section has 50 Multiple Choice Questions and covers topics like visual spatial reasoning, analytical reasoning, mental ability, and general awareness of architecture. It carries **200 marks**.
> 3.  **Part III: Drawing Test** - This section consists of 2 questions, designed to test your sketching ability, sense of proportion, perspective, and aesthetic sensitivity. It carries **100 marks**.
> 
> The total marks for JEE Paper 2 is **400 marks** (100 + 200 + 100). There is negative marking for incorrect answers in the Mathematics and Aptitude sections.
> 
> Are there any specific topics within these sections you'd like to know more about, or perhaps you're interested in how we cover this in our coaching?

### Turn 4 · 09 May 2026 12:01 IST

**Student asked:**

> can u give me the whole syllabus and each and every topic to be studied for nata

**Aintra answered:**

> The NATA exam covers a comprehensive syllabus designed to assess your aptitude for architecture. It's divided into two main parts: Part A (Drawing) and Part B (MCQ/NCQ). Here's a breakdown of the key areas and topics you should focus on:
> 
> **NATA 2026 Exam Pattern (Total 200 Marks, 3 Hours):**
> *   **Part A: Drawing (80 Marks, Offline)**
> *   **Part B: Multiple Choice Questions (MCQ) / Numerical Answer Questions (NCQ) (120 Marks, Online Adaptive)**
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Part A: Drawing (Offline - 80 Marks)
> 
> This section assesses your ability to sketch, visualize, and represent architectural concepts.
> **Key Topics:**
> 1.  **Composition and Design:**
>     *   Creating interesting compositions with given elements.
>     *   Understanding principles of design like balance, rhythm, proportion, unity, etc.
>     *   Designing functional and aesthetically pleasing spaces.
> 2.  **Sketching and Observation:**
>     *   Freehand sketching of objects, scenes, and people from memory or observation.
>     *   Drawing everyday objects, landscapes, or architectural elements with correct proportion and perspective.
> 3.  **Perspective Drawing:**
>     *   Understanding 1-point, 2-point, and 3-point perspective.
>     *   Drawing interior and exterior views of buildings or spaces.
>     *   Creating realistic depth and spatial relationships.
> 4.  **3D Perception and Visualization:**
>     *   Visualizing and drawing 3D forms from 2D views (plan, elevation).
>     *   Creating isometric and diametric projections.
>     *   Understanding shadows and light effects on objects.
> 5.  **Sense of Scale and Proportion:**
>     *   Representing objects and elements in their correct relative sizes.
>     *   Drawing human figures in proportion to buildings and spaces.
> 6.  **Colour Theory:**
>     *   Understanding primary, secondary, and tertiary colours.
>     *   Using colours to create mood, depth, and emphasis in drawings.
>     *   Knowledge of colour harmonies and contrasts.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Part B: MCQ / NCQ (Online Adaptive - 120 Marks)
> 
> This section tests your aptitude in various areas relevant to architecture.
> **Key Topics:**
> 1.  **Visual Reasoning:**
>     *   Analyzing patterns, shapes, and figures.
>     *   Identifying analogies and sequences.
>     *   Spatial reasoning and mental visualization.
> 2.  **Logical Reasoning:**
>     *   Deductive and inductive reasoning.
>     *   Problem-solving based on given information.
>     *   Critical thinking and analytical ability.
> 3.  **General Knowledge & Current Affairs (Related to Architecture):**
>     *   Knowledge of famous architects (Indian and International).
>     *   Iconic buildings and architectural styles (historical and contemporary).
>     *   Basic awareness of building materials, construction techniques, and environmental issues.
>     *   Current trends and developments in architecture and design.
> 4.  **Language Comprehension & Verbal Reasoning:**
>     *   Reading comprehension passages.
>     *   Vocabulary, grammar, and sentence correction.
>     *   Understanding and interpreting written information.
> 5.  **Mathematics (Basic level, up to 10+2 standard):**
>     *   Algebra (equations, basic functions).
>     *   Geometry (2D and 3D shapes, areas, volumes).
>     *   Trigonometry (basic concepts).
>     *   Mensuration.
>     *   Coordinate geometry.
>     *   Statistics and probability.
>     *   (Note: These are generally basic application-based questions, not complex calculus or advanced topics).
> 
> ---
> 
> **Important Notes for Self-Study:**
> *   **Practice is Key:** For both parts, consistent practice is crucial.
> *   **Time Management:** The NATA exam is 3 hours long. Practice completing sections within specific time limits.
> *   **Refer to the Official NATA Brochure:** The Council of Architecture (CoA) releases a detailed information brochure for each NATA session on **www.nata.in**. This is the most authoritative source for the exact syllabus and guidelines.
> 
> To help with your self-study, remember to utilize our **free tools at app.neramclasses.com**, especially the **Question Bank** and **Mock Tests**, which are designed to cover these topics.
> 
> Do you have any specific topics you'd like to dive deeper into, or would you like recommendations for practice resources?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 5555 ms_

> 
> However, we **do offer specialized training courses** in popular 3D modeling software:
>

>

>

### Turn 4 · 01 May 2026 12:31 IST

**Student asked:**

> 3D model making by foams

**Aintra answered:**

> That's an interesting question! You're asking about physical 3D model making using materials like foam, which is a fantastic skill for architects.
> 
> While physical 3D model making using foam or other materials is a crucial part of an architecture student's journey once they are in college, it is **not typically a direct part of our NATA or JEE Paper 2 entrance exam coaching curriculum.**
> 
> Our entrance exam coaching focuses on preparing you for the specific demands of the NATA and JEE Paper 2 exams, which include:
> *   **Part A (Drawing Test):** Freehand drawing, perspective, composition, 3D perception, and visualization skills.
> *   **Part B (MCQ/NCQ):** Mathematics, General Aptitude, and Logical Reasoning.
> 
> We develop your spatial reasoning and visualization skills through various drawing exercises and perspective studies, which are fundamental to understanding 3D forms. These skills are essential for both architectural drawing and eventually for physical model making.
> 
> So, while we don't do hands-on foam model making in our coaching, we build the foundational skills that will make you excellent at it once you get into an architecture program!
>

### Turn 2 · 27 Apr 2026 21:28 IST

**Student asked:**

> Ppl are saying the paper was moderate to easy

**Aintra answered:**

> You're absolutely right! When people say the paper was moderate to easy, it has a direct impact on percentiles.
> 
> Here's why:
> *   **Higher Scores Needed for High Percentiles:** If an exam is generally perceived as moderate to easy, it means more students tend to score higher marks. In such a scenario, to achieve a strong percentile (like 95th or 99th), you would typically need a significantly higher raw score compared to an exam that was considered difficult.
> *   **Competitive Landscape:** A moderate-to-easy paper makes the competition even tighter at the top. Even a small difference in marks can lead to a noticeable drop in percentile rank.
> 
> So, if the April 2026 JEE Paper 2 turns out to be moderate to easy, then for your target range of **86-120 marks**, the percentile might lean towards the lower end of the 85th-95th range, or even slightly below for the lower end of that score. To truly secure a top percentile (99+), you'd generally want to aim for scores well above 150-160, especially if the paper is on the easier side.
>

### Turn 4 · 18 Apr 2026 10:42 IST

**Student asked:**

> I have to give paper in June and august

**Aintra answered:**

> That's great you're planning your NATA attempts! It's important to understand how the NATA exam is structured.
> 
> For NATA 2026, the exam is conducted in two distinct phases:
> 
> 1.  **Phase 1 (April 4 – June 13, 2026):** In this phase, you can appear for **up to two attempts**. Your best score out of these attempts will be considered, and scoring is based on **percentiles**. If you want to participate in centralized counseling conducted by state or central governments, it's highly recommended to clear your attempts in Phase 1. This often leads to better opportunities and potentially lower fees through counseling.
> 2.  **Phase 2 (August 7-8, 2026):** This phase offers **one attempt only**, and scoring is based on **raw scores**. Students who appear in Phase 2 typically aim to clear the eligibility test to secure admission directly through institutions for vacant seats after centralized counseling concludes.
> 
> **Important Note:** You **cannot appear in both Phase 1 and Phase 2**. You must choose one phase to attempt the exam. Since you mentioned June and August, you would be looking at an attempt within Phase 1 (which runs until June 13) or the single attempt in Phase 2.
> 
> We generally advise students to target Phase 1 if they wish to participate in centralized counseling, as the most competitive colleges fill up through this process.
> 
> Do you have any questions about which phase might be more suitable for your goals?

### Turn 8 · 18 Apr 2026 09:27 IST

**Student asked:**

> How do you teach drawing online?

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a fantastic question, as drawing is such a hands-on skill! We've developed a highly effective methodology for teaching drawing online to ensure our students get the best possible guidance:
> 
> 1.  **Live Demonstrations:** Our instructors use digital drawing tablets and screensharing during live Microsoft Teams sessions. They can draw live on screen, demonstrating techniques, perspective, shading, and composition in real-time. This allows students to follow along step-by-step.
> 2.  **Interactive Feedback:** Students complete drawing assignments and then upload their work (usually by taking a clear photo) to our platform. Our faculty then provides personalized, detailed feedback. This often involves the instructor digitally drawing over the student's submitted work to correct lines, suggest improvements, or highlight areas for refinement, all explained verbally during live sessions or through recorded feedback.
> 3.  **Individual Attention:** Despite being online, our class sizes allow for individual attention. Students can share their screens, ask questions live, and receive direct critiques on their ongoing work.
> 4.  **Structured Curriculum:** We break down complex drawing concepts into manageable modules, from basic lines and shapes to advanced perspective, human figures, and composition, ensuring a progressive learning curve.
> 5.  **Recorded Sessions:** All live drawing classes are recorded and made available, so students can re-watch demonstrations and explanations as many times as needed for practice and revision.
> 
> This blend of live demonstration, interactive feedback, and structured learning ensures that our online drawing classes are just as effective, if not more so due to the flexibility, as traditional offline classes.

## Session 98: Guest (General)

**1** turn(s) · 17 Apr 2026 · `chat_1776384355018_lrlap80` · page: /colleges/tamil-nadu/anna-university-architecture

### Turn 1 · 17 Apr 2026 08:12 IST

**Student asked:**

> What is the syllabus of nata ?

**Aintra answered:**

> Hello there! The NATA exam syllabus is divided into two main parts, assessing different skills crucial for architecture.
> 
> **Part A (Drawing Test - 80 Marks):** This section is conducted offline and evaluates your drawing and observation skills. You'll be tested on your ability to sketch, perceive, analyze, and interpret visual compositions, including 3D understanding, perspective drawing, and understanding of scale and proportion.
> 
> **Part B (Multiple Choice Questions - 120 Marks):** This part is online and adaptive. It covers a range of subjects, including:
> *   **Mathematics:** Basic mathematical concepts relevant to design.
> *   **General Aptitude:** Problem-solving, logical reasoning, and analytical ability.
> *   **General Knowledge:** Current affairs, architectural awareness, and general awareness of the built environment.
> *   **Logical Reasoning:** Ability to deduce and infer from given information.
> 
> The entire exam is for **200 marks** and has a duration of **3 hours**. There is **no negative marking**, which is great news!
> 
> For a more detailed breakdown and to practice, you can visit our free tools at **app.neramclasses.com**, which includes a Question Bank and Mock Tests. You can also find comprehensive information at **neramclasses.com/nata-2026**.
> 
> Do you have any specific section of the syllabus you'd like to know more about?

> 4.  Once logged in, you should be able to view and download your admit card. Make sure to print a few copies for the exam day.
> 
> Please remember that Neram Classes is a coaching institute, and we do not issue admit cards directly. They are issued by the Council of Architecture (CoA) through the official NATA portal.

### Turn 3 · 10 Apr 2026 06:16 IST

**Student asked:**

> Ok I ah e doubt ... In part A we should do a 3d model know what type of questions they'll ask

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great question to clarify, and it's a common point of confusion!
> 
> In **Part A (Drawing Test)** of the NATA exam, you are **not expected to build a physical 3D model**. Instead, it's a drawing section where you will be asked to **draw or sketch** 3D objects, scenes, or concepts on paper.
> 
> The types of questions you can expect in Part A often involve:
> 
> *   **Perspective Drawing:** Drawing objects or scenes from a specific viewpoint, demonstrating your understanding of one-point, two-point, or even three-point perspective.
> *   **3D Composition:** Arranging various geometric forms (cubes, cylinders, cones, spheres) or everyday objects in a visually appealing 3D composition and rendering them with light and shadow.
> *   **Visualizing from 2D:** Sometimes you might be given 2D views (like top, front, side views) and asked to draw the 3D object, or vice-versa.
> *   **Scene Drawing:** Sketching an indoor or outdoor scene, incorporating human figures, furniture, or architectural elements, demonstrating your observation skills and ability to convey depth and atmosphere.
> *   **Design-related Sketching:** You might be asked to sketch a design for a specific object, space, or even a logo, showcasing your creativity and ability to represent ideas visually.
> 
> The key is to demonstrate your ability to visualize in three dimensions and represent that visualization accurately and artistically on a 2D surface (your drawing sheet). Focus on elements like proportion, perspective, light and shadow, and overall composition.
> 
> Don't worry about physical models; it's all about your drawing skills! You've got this. Just focus on clear lines, good proportions, and expressive shading.

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 3454 ms_  

### Turn 4 · 10 Apr 2026 06:16 IST

**Student asked:**

> A3 question

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a very practical question! When you hear "A3 question" in the context of the NATA Part A Drawing Test, it generally refers to the **size of the drawing sheet** that will be provided to you for that particular question.
> 
> *   **A3 is a standard paper size:** It measures **297 x 420 millimeters** (or approximately 11.7 x 16.5 inches).
> *   **Drawing Surface:** For the drawing questions in Part A, you will typically be provided with an A3 size sheet of paper to complete your drawing. This gives you a good amount of space to demonstrate your perspective, composition, and rendering skills.
> 
> So, it's not that the *question itself* is A3, but rather that you'll be answering the drawing prompt on an A3 sheet. Make sure to utilize the entire sheet effectively for your composition and details.
> 
> Are you feeling a bit more confident about what to expect for the drawing part now?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2724 ms_  

### Turn 5 · 10 Apr 2026 06:17 IST

**Student asked:**

> Part A 3rd question

**Aintra answered:**

> Ah, you're looking for a specific idea about what the **third question in Part A** might be! While I can't predict the exact question that will appear on your NATA exam tomorrow (as they change for each attempt and year), I can tell you about common types of "third questions" that often appear in NATA's Part A Drawing Test.
> 
> Typically, if there are multiple questions in Part A, the questions are designed to test different aspects of your drawing and visualization skills.
> 
> If the first two questions might cover things like:
> 1.  **Perspective drawing** (e.g., drawing a specific object or a street scene in 1 or 2-point perspective).
> 2.  **Composition with geometric or everyday objects** (e.g., arranging forms and rendering them with light and shadow).
> 
> Then, a **third question** often aims to assess your:
> 
> *   **Creativity and Imagination:** You might be asked to draw a scene based on a theme (e.g., "a futuristic city," "a bustling market," "a serene garden"), or to depict an abstract concept visually (e.g., "draw a scene representing 'movement'" or "illustrate 'balance'").
> *   **Observation and Memory Drawing:** Sometimes it involves sketching something you've seen or can imagine, like a specific type of building, a vehicle, or an interior space, often including human figures.
> *   **Design and Problem-Solving:** You could be asked to design and sketch an object, a piece of furniture, a small architectural element (like a bus stop or a kiosk), or even a logo, demonstrating your ability to translate an idea into a visual form.
> 
> The key for any Part A question, especially one that might be more open-ended, is to showcase your:
> *   **Clarity of thought**
> *   **Compositional skills**
> *   **Understanding of scale and proportion**
> *   **Ability to use light and shadow effectively**
> *   **Overall neatness and presentation**
> 
> Don't get too fixated on one specific type of question. Instead, be prepared to apply your core drawing skills to a variety of prompts that test your visualization, creativity, and rendering.
> 
> You've prepared for these skills, so trust yourself! Just read the question carefully tomorrow and let your creativity flow. Good luck!

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 6462 ms_  

### Turn 6 · 10 Apr 2026 06:19 IST

**Student asked:**

> A3 - 3D Composition-30 Marks: Creating interesting 3D composition for the given situation
> using the provided kit

**Aintra answered:**

> That's an excellent and very specific type of question for NATA Part A! It's great you're clarifying this.
> 
> This type of question, "A3 - 3D Composition-30 Marks: Creating interesting 3D composition for the given situation using the provided kit," is designed to test your spatial reasoning, creativity, and rendering skills within specific constraints.
> 
> Here's a breakdown of what it means and how to approach it:
> 
> 1.  **A3:** This confirms you'll be drawing on an A3 size sheet of paper.
> 2.  **3D Composition:** Your main task is to arrange various 3D forms in a visually appealing and stable way.
> 3.  **30 Marks:** This indicates it's a high-weightage question, so dedicate ample time (e.g., 40-50 minutes) and effort to it.
> 4.  **"for the given situation":** This is crucial. The question will provide a theme, context, or scenario. Your composition shouldn't just be random shapes; it needs to *interpret* or *represent* this situation.
>     *   **Examples:** "A monument for peace," "A children's play area," "A futuristic dwelling," "A sculpture representing growth," "A stage for a music concert." Your composition should evoke the feeling or function of the given situation.
> 5.  **"using the provided kit":** This is the most important constraint. You will be given a specific set of basic geometric solids (e.g., cubes, cylinders, cones, spheres, pyramids, cuboids, etc.). You **must only use these shapes** to create your composition. You might be asked to use a minimum number of each, or just to combine them creatively.
> 
> **How to approach this question tomorrow:**
> 
> *   **Read Carefully:** Understand the "situation" and identify exactly what forms are in the "kit."
> *   **Brainstorm & Sketch Thumbnails:** Don't jump straight to the final drawing. Quickly sketch 2-3 small ideas on the side of your sheet (if allowed, or in your mind). How can these abstract forms represent the given situation?
>     *   Think about **balance, rhythm, hierarchy, and a focal point**.
>     *   How do the forms interact? Are they stacked, interlocked, intersecting, or balanced on each other?
> *   **Choose a Perspective:** Select a dynamic perspective (usually 2-point perspective is effective) that best showcases your composition and its relationship to the ground plane.
> *   **Draw with Clarity:** Use clean lines. Pay attention to proportion and scale between the forms.
> *   **Light and Shadow:** This is critical! Apply consistent light and shadow to give your composition depth and volume. Choose a single light source and make sure the shadows fall correctly on all surfaces and cast shadows on the ground plane.
> *   **Ground Plane:** Always include a ground plane to anchor your composition and make it feel like it exists in space.
> 
> This question tests your ability to think abstractly, solve a visual problem with given constraints, and render it convincingly in 3D. Trust your ability to manipulate forms and interpret the theme.
> 
> You've got this! Just stay calm and focused. Good luck for tomorrow!

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 8881 ms_

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 3649 ms_  

### Turn 2 · 09 Apr 2026 08:23 IST

**Student asked:**

> The more questions we make mistakes the less marks we will get?

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great question!
> 
> For the NATA exam, there is **no negative marking**. This means you will not lose marks for incorrect answers. You only gain marks for correct responses, and there's no penalty for mistakes. So, it's always a good idea to attempt all questions!
> 
> Do you have any other questions about the NATA exam or how to prepare effectively?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 1734 ms_  

### Turn 3 · 09 Apr 2026 08:26 IST

**Student asked:**

> Now I made a question wrong nxt question will be easier than the before one know then the question will be marked as wrong or instead of that question the easier question I wrote is correct t

**Aintra answered:**

> That's an excellent point about the adaptive nature of NATA Part B!
> 
> You're right that NATA Part B is an **adaptive test**. This means the difficulty of the questions you receive can adjust based on your performance. If you answer a question incorrectly, the system might present an easier question next to gauge your understanding. Conversely, if you answer correctly, it might present a harder one.
> 
> However, how it's marked is straightforward:
> *   **Each question is scored individually.** If you answer a question incorrectly, you simply don't get marks for *that specific question*.
> *   The **next question's difficulty changes**, but its mark is independent. If an easier question is presented and you answer it correctly, you'll receive marks for *that* correct answer. It doesn't retroactively change the mark of the previous incorrect question.
> *   Remember, there's **no negative marking** in NATA. So, making a mistake on one question doesn't deduct marks from your total score; it just means you don't earn marks for that particular question.
> 
> The adaptive system helps the exam determine your proficiency level more accurately by tailoring the questions to your ability, but the scoring for each question remains independent.
> 
> Does that clarify how the adaptive system works with scoring?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 4167 ms_  

### Turn 4 · 09 Apr 2026 08:27 IST

**Student asked:**

> So on the end the questions whichever questions I have attempted will be correct

**Aintra answered:**

> Thanks for asking for clarification!
> 
> No, that's not quite how it works. At the end, **only the questions you have answered *correctly* will contribute to your score.**
> 
> The adaptive nature of the test means that the difficulty of the questions you see *changes* as you progress. So, if you answer a difficult question incorrectly, the next question might be easier. If you answer an easy question correctly, the next might be harder.
> 
> But regardless of whether a question was easy or hard, or if it came after a correct or incorrect answer:
> *   If you answer a question **correctly**, you get marks for it.
> *   If you answer a question **incorrectly**, you get **0 marks** for it (but no negative marks are deducted).
> 
> Your final score will be the sum of all the marks from the questions you answered correctly throughout the test.
> 
> I hope this makes it clearer! Do you have any other questions?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 3429 ms_

**Student asked:**

> In part b

**Aintra answered:**

> Thanks for clarifying!
> 
> For **Part B** of the NATA exam:
> 
> *   This section carries a total of **120 marks**.
> *   It consists of Multiple Choice Questions (MCQs) and Numerical Answer Questions (NCQs).
> *   **Crucially, Part B is an online adaptive test.** This means the difficulty and the marks allocated to each question can vary based on your performance as you progress through the test. So, there isn't a fixed "X marks per question" for every single question in Part B.
> *   Just like the overall exam, there is **no negative marking** in Part B.
> 
> This adaptive nature helps assess a candidate's abilities more accurately.
> 
> Does that help clarify the marking for Part B?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 3157 ms_  

### Turn 4 · 09 Apr 2026 07:53 IST

**Student asked:**

> I don't understand how does that work

**Aintra answered:**

> I understand, the "online adaptive test" concept can be a bit tricky to grasp! Let me explain it simply.
> 
> Imagine you're taking a test, and the computer is smart.
> 
> 1.  **If you answer a question correctly**, the system might then give you a slightly **harder** question next. A harder question might be worth a bit more credit or contribute more significantly to your overall score.
> 2.  **If you answer a question incorrectly**, the system might then give you a slightly **easier** question. An easier question might be worth less credit.
> 
> So, instead of everyone getting the exact same set of questions in the same order, and each question being worth the same fixed number of marks, the test *adapts* to your performance. The goal is to figure out your skill level more efficiently by adjusting the questions presented to you.
> 
> This means the **total 120 marks for Part B** are distributed across the questions you answer, but the "value" or impact of each individual question can vary depending on its difficulty and how you're performing throughout the test.
> 
> Does that explanation make a bit more sense?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 3267 ms_  

### Turn 5 · 09 Apr 2026 07:54 IST

**Student asked:**

> Ohhh ok thank you

**Aintra answered:**

> You're very welcome! I'm glad that explanation helped clarify how the adaptive testing works for Part B of NATA.
> 
> Is there anything else I can assist you with regarding NATA, JEE Paper 2, or Neram Classes? Feel free to ask!

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 1267 ms_

**Aintra answered:**

> Hi there! That's a great question, but there might be a slight misunderstanding about the NATA exam schedule. The NATA exam isn't conducted in weekly sessions. Instead, it's held in specific attempts spread across two main phases throughout the year.
> 
> For NATA 2026, here's how it works:
> *   **Phase 1** will have up to **2 attempts** between **April 4 and June 13, 2026**.
> *   **Phase 2** will have **1 attempt** on **August 7-8, 2026**.
> 
> You can choose to appear in either Phase 1 or Phase 2, but not both. We highly recommend aiming for Phase 1 to take advantage of the multiple attempts and percentile scoring!

### Turn 3 · 06 Apr 2026 19:48 IST

**Student asked:**

> Actually I am asking NATA exam is conducted on each Friday and Saturday I guess Saturdays 2:15 and Friday has one session for booking the date and session for the exam shajapur the NATA browse jail I want to know like how many sessions on Friday and how many sessions on Saturday exam can be booked for the NATA exam

**Aintra answered:**

> My apologies for the misunderstanding! Thanks for clarifying that you're asking about the NATA *exam booking sessions* on specific days.
> 
> You're right that when you register for the NATA exam on www.nata.in, you'll be able to choose your preferred exam date and time slot (session) at your chosen test center. While the NATA exam window extends over several weeks, the exams aren't necessarily conducted *every* Friday and Saturday. Instead, the Council of Architecture (CoA) designates specific dates within those windows when the exam will be held.
> 
> When you go to book your slot on the NATA portal:
> *   You will see a calendar showing the **available exam dates**. These dates will fall within the official NATA exam windows (e.g., Phase 1: April 4 – June 13, 2026).
> *   For each available date, there are typically **two sessions per day** offered at most test centers:
>     *   One in the **morning**
>     *   One in the **afternoon**
> *   You can select the date and session (morning or afternoon) that works best for you, depending on availability at your chosen center.
> 
> The exact number of available sessions on a specific Friday or Saturday will depend on the overall exam schedule released by CoA and the capacity of the test centers. It's always best to check the official NATA registration portal (www.nata.in) once registration opens to see the precise dates and session availability for your preferred location.
> 
> Does that help clarify how you can book your NATA exam sessions?

## Session 143: Guest (General)

**2** turn(s) · 03 Apr 2026 · `chat_1775174310871_o7sxqqc` · page: /apply

### Turn 1 · 03 Apr 2026 05:28 IST

**Student asked:**

> Hi

**Aintra answered:**

> Hi there! Welcome to Neram Classes. How can I help you today? 😊

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2213 ms_  

### Turn 2 · 03 Apr 2026 05:30 IST

**Student asked:**

> In nata examination the prase 1 eligible after the prase 2 will be written is compulsory

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great question about the NATA exam!
> 
> No, if you are eligible in Phase 1, writing Phase 2 is **not compulsory**. In fact, you **cannot appear in both** Phase 1 and Phase 2. You have to choose which phase you want to attempt.
> 
> Here's a quick breakdown:
> *   **Phase 1 (April 4 – June 13, 2026)**: This phase allows for up to two attempts, and your scores are given as percentiles. We highly recommend appearing in Phase 1 if you intend to participate in centralized counseling conducted by state or central governments. Most competitive colleges fill their seats through this counseling process, and you might get better fee structures.
> *   **Phase 2 (August 7-8, 2026)**: This phase offers one attempt, and your scores are raw scores. This is generally for students who wish to clear the eligibility test to approach institutions directly for vacant seats after the counseling process is complete.
> 
> So, you should decide which phase aligns best with your application strategy. For the best chances at top colleges and through centralized counseling, Phase 1 is generally recommended.
> 
> Do you have any other questions about the NATA exam?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 4606 ms_

## Session 159: Guest (General)

**3** turn(s) · 26 Mar 2026 · `chat_1774467348251_ld6484x` · page: /nata-2026/how-to-apply

### Turn 1 · 26 Mar 2026 01:06 IST

**Student asked:**

> Can u explain me clearly about nata phase 1 exam has 2 attempts

**Aintra answered:**

> Certainly! Let me explain the NATA Phase 1 exam attempts clearly.
> 
> The NATA exam, conducted by the Council of Architecture (CoA), is typically held in two phases. For **NATA Phase 1 (usually from April to June)**, students are given the opportunity to appear for **up to two attempts**.
> 
> Here's how it works:
> 1.  You can register for the NATA Phase 1 exam and take one attempt.
> 2.  If you wish to improve your score, you have the option to register and appear for a **second attempt** within the Phase 1 window.
> 3.  The Council of Architecture will then consider your **best score out of these two attempts** for your final NATA Phase 1 result. This allows students a chance to perform better if their first attempt didn't go as planned.
> 
> It's important to note that NATA Phase 1 scores are usually based on a **percentile scoring** system. There's also a Phase 2 of the NATA exam (usually in August) which has only one attempt and uses raw scores, but you cannot appear in both Phase 1 and Phase 2. We generally recommend aiming for Phase 1 if you want to participate in centralized counseling.
> 
> Do you have any other questions about the NATA exam or our coaching programs? You can also check out our free NATA tools at app.neramclasses.com!

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 4085 ms_  

### Turn 2 · 26 Mar 2026 01:09 IST

**Student asked:**

> If I choose phase 1 when can write the two attempts

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great question! If you choose to appear for NATA Phase 1, you can write your two attempts within the designated Phase 1 examination window.
> 
> For **NATA 2026, Phase 1 is scheduled from April 4 to June 13, 2026**. Within this broad period, you will typically be able to choose specific dates for your first and second attempts based on slot availability when you register. You don't necessarily have to take them consecutively; you can space them out within that window to allow for preparation between attempts.
> 
> Remember, the Council of Architecture will consider your **best score out of those two attempts** for your final Phase 1 result.
> 
> Always keep an eye on the official NATA website (www.nata.in) for the exact registration opening and closing dates for each attempt, as well as the specific exam dates available for booking.
> 
> Is there anything else I can clarify about the NATA exam or our coaching at Neram Classes?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2930 ms_  

### Turn 3 · 26 Mar 2026 01:10 IST

**Student asked:**

> If we register for first attempt and second attempt separately

**Aintra answered:**

> That's an excellent clarifying question!
> 
> When you register for NATA Phase 1, your **initial registration fee typically covers the opportunity to appear for up to two attempts** within that phase. You generally **don't need to pay a separate registration fee for the second attempt** if you're taking both within the same NATA Phase 1 cycle.
> 
> However, while the registration covers the *option* for two attempts, you will usually **schedule (or book) your exam dates for each attempt separately** through the NATA applicant portal. This means you might book your first attempt, take it, and then later decide to book your second attempt within the Phase 1 window if you want to improve your score.
> 
> So, think of it as one registration that grants you two chances to perform your best in NATA Phase 1!
> 
> Does that make sense? Let me know if you have more questions!

👍 Marked as a good answer

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a very practical question for NATA preparation!
> 
> The NATA exam is divided into two parts: Part A (Drawing) and Part B (MCQ/NCQ). Mathematics questions fall under **Part B**, which is an online adaptive test worth **120 marks**.
> 
> While the exact number of mathematics questions can vary slightly as Part B is an adaptive test, it typically includes questions from:
> *   **Mathematics**
> *   General Aptitude
> *   Logical Reasoning
> *   Architectural Awareness
> 
> There isn't a fixed, publicly released number of questions allocated specifically to Maths for every single attempt, as the adaptive nature means the test adjusts based on your performance. However, you can expect a significant portion of Part B to be dedicated to Maths and Aptitude.

**Student asked:**

> Is nata is for joining private or government college

**Aintra answered:**

> NATA (National Aptitude Test in Architecture) is the primary entrance examination for admission to **both government and private architecture colleges** across India.
> 
> If you want to pursue a Bachelor of Architecture (B.Arch) degree, you generally need a valid NATA score to be eligible for admission to most architecture colleges, whether they are government-funded institutions, state universities, or private colleges. Each college will then have its own admission criteria, often combining your NATA score with your 10+2 academic performance.
> 
> For some top government institutions like IITs, NITs, and SPAs, you might also need to appear for **JEE Main Paper 2** (and potentially AAT for IITs). However, NATA remains a widely accepted and often mandatory eligibility test for the vast majority of B.Arch programs.
> 
> So, in short, NATA is crucial for getting into almost any B.Arch program, regardless of whether it's a government or private college.
> 
> Does that clarify how NATA fits into college admissions?

While a high NATA score (like 150+) is excellent and will open doors to many other top government and private colleges, it is generally **not accepted by NITs** for their B.Arch admissions.
> 
> You would need to appear for and score well in JEE Main Paper 2 to be eligible for B.Arch programs at NITs, SPAs (Schools of Planning and Architecture), and some other centrally funded technical institutions.

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a great question! Understanding the differences between NATA Phase 1 and Phase 2 is important for planning your exam strategy.
> 
> Here are the main distinctions for NATA 2026:
> 
> *   **Phase 1:** This phase runs from **April 4 to June 13, 2026**. Students can attempt the exam up to **two times** during this period, and the scoring is based on a **percentile system**.
> *   **Phase 2:** This phase is scheduled for **August 7-8, 2026**. Only **one attempt** is allowed in Phase 2, and the scoring is based on **raw scores**.
> 
> **Important Note:** You **cannot appear in both** Phase 1 and Phase 2. You must choose which phase you wish to attempt.
> 
> For more detailed information on NATA, you can always visit our NATA 2026 page at neramclasses.com/nata-2026 or use our free tools at app.neramclasses.com!

### Turn 2 · 21 Mar 2026 14:25 IST

**Student asked:**

> What do u recommend me  whether  I could phase 1 or phase 2

**Aintra answered:**

> That's a very good question, and the best choice often depends on your individual preparation level and strategy!
> 
> Considering the structure of NATA 2026:
> 
> *   **Phase 1 (April 4 – June 13, 2026):** This phase allows you up to **two attempts**. This is a significant advantage because it gives you a chance to learn from your first attempt and improve your score on the second. The percentile scoring also means your performance is evaluated relative to other test-takers, which can sometimes be beneficial.
> *   **Phase 2 (August 7-8, 2026):** This phase offers only **one attempt**, and the scoring is based on raw scores. While it gives you more time to prepare, the single attempt makes it higher risk, as you don't get a second chance to improve your score if something goes wrong.
> 
> **Our Recommendation:**
> 
> For most students, we generally **recommend aiming for Phase 1**. The primary reason is the opportunity for **two attempts**. This allows you to gain valuable exam experience, identify areas for improvement after your first try, and then apply those learnings to score better in your second attempt. It also means you'll have your results earlier, which can be helpful for college applications and counseling.
> 
> However, if you feel you need significant additional preparation time and are confident in performing well in a single attempt, Phase 2 could be an option. But for maximizing your chances, Phase 1 with its multiple attempts is usually the safer and more strategic choice.
> 
> Are you looking to start your NATA preparation soon? We offer comprehensive online and offline coaching to help you excel!

## Session 182: Guest (General)

**3** turn(s) · 20 Mar 2026 · `chat_1773999771290_k0fwg25` · page: /nata-2026

### Turn 1 · 20 Mar 2026 15:13 IST

**Student asked:**

> In nata exam there two sessions

**Aintra answered:**

> Yes, the NATA exam is conducted in two phases!
> 
> Here's a quick breakdown:
> *   **Phase 1:** This phase runs from **April 4 to June 13, 2026**. You can attempt the exam up to **2 times** during this period, and your scores will be evaluated based on a **percentile** system.
> *   **Phase 2:** This phase is held on **August 7-8, 2026**. You get **1 attempt** in this phase, and scores are based on **raw scores**.
> 
> It's important to note that you **cannot appear in both phases**. The exam is conducted by the Council of Architecture (CoA).
> 
> For more detailed information, you can always visit the official NATA website at www.nata.in or check out our NATA chatbot and resources at neramclasses.com/nata-2026.
> 
> Do you have any other questions about the NATA exam structure?

_model: gemini-2.5-flash · 2944 ms_  

### Turn 2 · 20 Mar 2026 15:14 IST

**Student asked:**

> I can't understand clearly

**Aintra answered:**

> Apologies! Let me try to explain it more simply.
> 
> Think of it like two different "rounds" or "windows" for taking the NATA exam in a year:
> 
> 1.  **Phase 1 (Early Exams):**
>     *   This is the first opportunity to take the NATA exam.
>     *   It happens over a longer period, from **April 4 to June 13, 2026**.
>     *   During this phase, you can try the exam up to **two times**. The Council of Architecture (CoA) will consider your best score.
>     *   Your score in this phase is given as a **percentile**, which shows how you performed compared to other students.
> 
> 2.  **Phase 2 (Later Exam):**
>     *   This is a second, separate opportunity to take the NATA exam.
>     *   It's held on specific dates: **August 7-8, 2026**.
>     *   In this phase, you only get **one attempt**.
>     *   Your score here is a **raw score** (the actual marks you achieved).
> 
> **The most important thing to remember is:** You have to choose either Phase 1 OR Phase 2. You **cannot appear in both** Phase 1 and Phase 2.
> 
> Does that make more sense? Let me know if you have more questions!

**Aintra answered:**

> The NATA exam syllabus is divided into two main parts:
> 
> **Part A: Drawing (80 Marks, Offline)**
> This section assesses your drawing and observation skills. It typically includes questions related to:
> *   **Aesthetic Sensitivity:** Understanding of scale, proportion, color, and composition.
> *   **Visual Perception:** Ability to perceive and represent 3D objects from 2D drawings.
> *   **Imagination & Creativity:** Drawing from memory, creating compositions, and expressing ideas visually.
> *   **Drawing Aptitude:** Sketching, rendering, and understanding perspectives.
> 
> **Part B: Multiple Choice Questions (MCQ) & Numerical Answer Type (NAT) Questions (120 Marks, Online Adaptive)**
> This section covers a broader range of topics, including:
> *   **General Aptitude:** Logical reasoning, verbal ability, numerical ability, and data interpretation.
> *   **Architecture & Design Awareness:** Knowledge of famous architectural works, architects, building materials, and general design principles.
> *   **Mathematics:** Basic concepts from 10+2 level, including algebra, trigonometry, geometry, and calculus.
> *   **Physics:** Basic concepts from 10+2 level.
> 
> The NATA exam is conducted in **English and Hindi ONLY**. There is no negative marking, and the total duration is 3 hours.
> 
> For more detailed information and to practice questions, you can check out our free Question Bank at **app.neramclasses.com**.
